 PART A Introduction 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 1 . Preface The work of editing St. Birgitta's Sermo Angelicus was begun about i o years ago by a member of the Institute of Classical Languages in Uppsala, Henrik Leby. He checked all the MSS he could find, listed the errors and miswritings in them and produced a preliminary stemma codicum. The results of his investigations were collected in a typewritten dissertation for the Swedish licentiate degree. In 1967 another member of the Institute, Birger Bergh, published Book VII of St. Birgitta's Revelations and this was of some importance also as regarded the Sermo Angelicus text, since the latter has to some extent been handed down in the same MSS as Book VII. In 197o Prof. Sven Lundström, of Uppsala, started a project for editing Latin texts dating from the Middle Ages in Sweden. Money was provided by the Swedish Humanistic Research Council and I was entrusted with the task of editing the Sermo Angelicus. Leby's dissertation was my starting-point in preparing this edition and subsec- tions 4.1.1-5 and section 4.3 of the present book are based mainly on an evaluation of the material collected by him. Leby had access to all the MSS (or microfilms of them) of the Revelations and Breviary traditions (with the exception of Aa oa Ab j v Fb Jb, which were not known to him). The films were not available to me, nor were Leby's complete collations; I have had to obtain fresh microfilms and, in doing this, I have chosen only the most important MSS. This means that, in work- ing out the detailed stemma picture of the Revelations and Breviary traditions (subsections 4.1.1-5 and section 4.3), I have had to fall back upon an evaluation of the material available in Leby's dissertation. This, however, does not seem to have been any disadvantage, since there is no reason for assuming that Leby overlooked any important errors; in fact, he read the MSS fairly carefully, as far as I have been able to check him. The rest of the edition—everything, except subsections 4.1.1-5 and section 4.3—is the result of new research. I have received assistance from fil. kand. Anders Piltz, of Uppsala, who collated MSS r 0 Za y Te (I myself am responsible for the collation of L Ad F Fe je Aa oa Ab j v Fb Jb and for the sporadic checks made in other MSS) and collected some of the quotations and reminiscences in the text. Uppsala, December 1971 Sten Eklund 
 
 
 
 
 2. Lists of manuscripts, printed literature and abbreviations I give here lists of the manuscripts, the printed literature and, at the end, some abbreviations. As regards the dates of the MSS, it should be observed that I do not go into details and that more information and discussions are often provided in the literature cited for each MS. The same reservation is to be made also as regards the contents of the MSS; for exhaustive information I refer to the literature quoted. I indicate the position of the SA text in those MSS in which it may be difficult to find; when folio numbers are not given, this means that it is easy to locate the SA text. I also indicate gaps in the SA text itself (not as regards the prologue, incipit, headings and absolutions), but I give only the paragraph numbers without specify- ing the first and last missing words (for information of this kind, as regards the MSS used in establishing the text, I refer the reader to the apparatus). As regards the list of printed literature, I have included there only books which are referred to by abbreviated titles somewhere in this edition. Two figures separated by a colon (for example, 21: 26) refer to chapter and paragraph, two figures separated by a comma (for example, 21, 84) refer to chapter and line. Passages in the eight books containing St. Birgitta's Revelations are denoted by the abbreviation Rev., followed by the number of the book and the chapter (for example, Rev. I 3), except for Book VII, for which the paragraph numbers of Bergh's edition are also given (for example, Rev. VII 31: 13). Quotations from the Reuelaciones extrauagantes are preceded by the abbreviation Ex., followed by the number of the chapter and paragraph (Hollman's edition; for example, Ex. 116: 5). Or. stands for the Quattuor oraciones (the Ghotan edition). Latin texts (including the Vulgate) which are listed in the TLL index are referred to by the abbreviations used in the TLL. The Missale Romanum is quoted from the Rome edition of 1920 and the Breviarium Romanum from the Regensburg edition of 1865-66 (see the list of printed literature). 3. The history of the text According to the prologue, the SA was revealed to St. Birgitta while she was staying in a house adjoining the church of San Lorenzo in Damaso in Rome. This must have been after the year when she arrived in Rome (about 135o). It is dif- ficult to get a more accurate dating, but the problem has been attacked by Collins (pp. XXIII if.), who has called attention to some important points. Among other 
 
 
 
 
 19 things, he says (pp. XXIX f.) that the SA must have existed in 1366 and for this he refers to Ex. 113, in which St. Mary tells St. Birgitta to send the horas to a friend (perhaps Prior Petrus); he in turn was to show them to Bishop Henning, of Åbo in Finland. In connection with this the lecciones ( = the SA) are also mentioned. Since Bishop Henning died in 1366, the SA probably existed by then. Thus it seems to have been revealed to St. Birgitta between the years 135o and 1366; the exact year is subject to discussion, but Collins (p. xxvii I) assumes that it was either 1353 or 1354. I may add the following remark to Collins's arguments. In the D eposicio copiosissima (A&P pp. 526 f.) there is a version of the prologue to the SA which is somewhat different from the one given in the SA MSS. It can be seen from this version (as Collins also observes) that Prior Petrus was in Sweden when the SA was revealed to St. Birgitta and that Magister Petrus was her confessor at that time (the dominus Petrus mentioned must be Magister Petrus, since it is Prior Petrus who is testifying). Now, Schiick (VHAAH 33, NF 13, 1, 1901, p. 15) has found that Prior Petrus visited Sweden in the years 1354, 1365 and 1367 (perhaps also 136o) and accordingly (if Schäck is right in this) the SA was revealed to St. Birgitta in one of these years; the year 1354 is in accordance with the results gained by Collins. Klockars (p. 48) has on this ground assumed that it was 1354. The SA was "revealed" to St. Birgitta in Swedish and translated into Latin; this translation was probably carried out by one of her confessors, Magister Petrus. Since Prior Petrus was in Sweden (cf. above) at that time, it is likely from this fact that Magister Petrus was the translator. There is also—as Collins pointed out on pp. xviii f, positive evidence for this in the Myroure of Oure Ladye.1 The 1 For information on The Myroure I refer to the edition by J. H. Blunt (The Myroure of Oure Ladye ... ed. J. H. Blunt, Early English Text Society, Extra Series no. XIX, London 1873) and Collins, pp. xxxv ff. The content is described thus by Collins: "After two prologues, a treatise `wherein ys shewed the condycion of diuine seruyce', and a short essay on the `Deuoute redyng of holy Bokes', The Myroure translates, with a wealth of explanation and comment, the entire text of the Breviary (apart from the psalms), supplying, in addition, renderings of the Masses of Our Lady." It is not known for certain when The Myroure was composed. There remain a few copies of the editio princeps of 1530 and a MS (defective) which Blunt took to have been written between 1460 and 1500 (he refers to the kind of handwriting in doing so), whereas Collins says the beginning of the i 6th century. Both Blunt and Collins assume that The Myroure was composed during the first half of the i 5th century, although they diller in their opinions as to the author; thus it was probably written less than a hundred years after the death of St. Birgitta and the information pro- vided by it may very weil be of substantial value. There are the following passages in The Myroure which confirm the idea that Magister Petrus carried out the translation (Blunt's edition, pp. 18-2o): How an angell was sente of god to saynte Byrgyt to endyte youre Legende in the tongue of Swethe whyche mayster Peter drew fyrste into Latyn ... For whan saint Byrgytte had wryten the Legende of the Aungells mouthe in her owne tongue; then the aungell bad her take yt to master Peter for to drawe yt in to latyn sayde thus vnto her : "..." Whan mayster Peter had turned thys Legende in to latyn out of the tongue of Swethe, for he was a man borne of the same londe ; then he sent yt to master Al- phonse whyche was a doctoure of dyuynyte to whom our lorde bad that all the reuelacyons shulde be taken after they were drawen in to latyn, for the same Alphonse was borne in Spayne, many an honderyth myle from Swethe, and coulde nothynge of her language. And thefore they neded to be drawen in to latyn or he se them. To hym the lessons and reuelacyons were taken, that he shulde se that they were sett in trew and conuenyente termes, wythout erroure or darknes, for he was a greate clerke. 
 
 
 
 
 20 Myroure also mentions that Magister Petrus showed his rendering to the Spanish bishop, Alfonso da Vadaterra, so that he should see that it was in accordance with the faith. Thus we are entitled to assume that the SA was translated by Magister Petrus and checked by Alfonso. Yet it is likely that the prologue was not composed by Magister Petrus but was written by Alfonso (p. 58, note 31). The SA has come down to us in three main ways, each represented by a certain number of MSS. First of all, it has been handed down together with, the other Revelations of St. Birgitta. Secondly, it is part of the Breviary composed for the nuns of Vadstena. This Breviary was finished by Magister Petrus (Westman, p. 18; Collins, p. XIX; Klockars, pp. 48 f.), who added the liturgical parts to the SA. The third way by which the SA text has been transmitted to us is via the Celeste Viridarium (henceforth the abbreviation CV will be used). This is a com- pilation from the Revelations made by Alfonso (the information that Alfonso was responsible for it is provided by the Incipit of cod. L [see Schiick's printed text of the cod. L Incipit in VHAAH 33, NF 13, 1, 1901, p. 48]), in which are brought together all the revelations concerning St. Mary. The CV contains the SA in extenso, although it has been split up into several minor parts, between which are placed other shorter or longer pieces of text. Westman (p. 45) has assumed that Alfonso composed the CV in the 138os and, although there is no absolute evidence for this, it seems likely.

 23 4. The manuscripts It is clear from the survey in the preceding chapter that the SA MSS may be distributed in three groups, viz. the Revelations, the Breviary and the CV MSS. This distribution has been made with regard to the contents of the MSS (it can be seen from the lists in Chapter 2 to which group each MS belongs). It is characteristic of the Revelations MSS that they generally contain also other parts of St. Birgitta's Revelations, whereas this is generally not so with the Breviary MSS. The Revelations MSS generally also have the prologue and the headings for the seven days of the week; these parts are generally missing in the Breviary MSS. The Breviary MSS, on the other hand, have the hymns, antiphons and responds composed by Magister Petrus intermingled with the SA text; these parts are, of course, not handed down in the Revelations MSS. The two CV MSS are specific in so far as the SA text is split up in a very specific way and is part of a major compilation from the Revelations corpus. This grouping is based on external features. The possibility must not be over- looked that there may have been some interference between these three groups, for instance, the SA text of some Breviary MS may have been copied from a Revelations MS and vice versa. In fact, there is some interference of this kind between the Breviary and the Revelations MSS.2 There are three Breviary MSS, Ac Be Cc, the SA text of which is apparently more closely connected with that of the Revelations MSS than that of the other Breviary MSS. On the contrary, the SA text of Revelations MS b is apparently taken from a Breviary MS and not from another Revelations MS. These statements are based on internal criteria, viz. errors in the MSS, and will be accounted for later on. As an appropriate terminology, I have introduced the terms "Breviary tradition", "Revelations tradition" and "CV tradition". By "Breviary tradition" I mean the Breviary MSS, with the exception of Ac Be Cc, and the Revelations MS b; the term "Revelations tradition" refers to the Revelations MSS, with the exception of b, and the Breviary MSS Ae Be Cc; the term "CV tradition" is reserved for the CV MSS. It may be added that there are a few other Breviary MSS (De Be Me I\Te) which have indeed some connection with the Revelations tradition, although this connection is not so marked as to justify transferring them from the Breviary tradition to the Revelations tradition. It may be convenient to add here some technical remarks concerning the fol- lowing pages. The MSS have been classified according to the errors which they contain. These errors are basically of two different kinds. First, there are cases in which it is apparent from the context or the syntax that the text of some MS or MSS is incorrect. Take, for example, the passage in 2: 2, in which the words nouit 2 There was apparently no such interference as regarded the two CV MSS and this is quite natural. It would have been impossible to recompose the SA from a CV MS without the aid of a Revelations or Breviary MS, nor was it likely that the scattered parts of the SA in the CV would have been collated with a Revelations or Breviary MS. 
 
 
 
 
 24 Deus sua are not found in MS c; this is apparently an error, since the content does not make sense without these words. Secondly, there are errors of the fol- lowing kind: in 2: 14 the MSS Ya B have autem instead of eciam and this is an error connecting Ya B. However, the judgement that eciam is correct and autem an error cannot be made with reference to the content or syntax but only by a comparison with the other MSS. Since autem is found only in ya B and eciam in the other MSS of the p, group (to which ya B belong), as weil as in all the other MSS of the Revelations, Breviary and CV traditions, it must have been in the archetype and accordingly autem is an error. There is, of course, no strict boundary between these two kinds of errors; an error of the former kind is generally at the same time an error from the second point of view. Now, errors of the former kind are, of course, generally the most "reliable" ones but the latter type must also be taken into account (especially in the SA text, in which errors of the former kind are rare).3 In the lists of errors in the following pages the passages are given in numerical order; I have not tried to group them in accordance with the distinction just made or with regard to their value, since such groupings would be more or less arbitrary, but have restricted myself to some remarks on the value of the errors. It is necessary to make sure that the errors which are used to group the MSS are only in the MSS under discussion. In every passage I have adduced in sections 4.1-4.3, it is possible to draw the following conclusion e silentio: the correct reading, which is in the text I have established, is in all the MSS which are not especially mentioned (sometimes for the sake of clarity I have nevertheless re- marked that the correct reading is in some MSS; in such cases the correct reading is also in all the MSS not mentioned). This means that, when I say, for example, that in 2: 14 there is autem in ya B instead of eciam, the correct reading is eciam and this is in all the MSS of the three traditions, with the exception of ya B.4 When other MS(S) than those under discussion have another error than that discussed, I have generally mentioned this within parentheses (I have omitted this information on a few occasions when it was absolutely clear that the two errors had nothing to do with each other), for example, i o: i 1 eciam nulla eos Je Ke b instead of nulla eciam eos (eos eciam nulla Uc). I have used Greek letters in the stemma to denote lost MSS from which two or 3 There are, on the whole, few "good errors" in the SA MSS. In the MSS of the Breviary tra- dition, the errors are so rare and trifling that it was impossible to produce a detailed stemma. Also as regards the MSS of the Revelations tradition, the material is sometimes meagre, so that one has to fall back upon errors which are not really striking (this, of course, implies a lower degree of reliability). 4 There are the following exceptions to this rule. (a) When any MS lacks a major part of the text to which the passage under discussion belongs, this is not mentioned. (b) On a few occasions the material offered by Leby's dissertation (see the Preface) was insufficient and I had to check the MSS myself. Since I did not have access to all of them, I cannot in these cases answer for the entire mass of MSS. Whenever I have used such a passage, this has been noted. (c) The recently discovered MSS dealt with in section 4.5 have not been checked in this way. 
 
 
 
 
 25 more groups of MSS (sometimes also single extant MSS) are derived. Whenever I give a reading followed by such a Greek letter, this means that I have checked that all the extant MSS which are derived (indirectly via other lost MSS or directly) from the lost MS designated by the Greek letter have this reading. For example, when I say that 131 and P2 have intrauit in 2: 13 instead of intraret, this means that I have checked that intrauit is in all the p, and (32 MSS, viz. r V Y P o 0 e ya B d m c. This rule applies to the introduction as well as to the ap- paratus criticus.5 4.1. The Revelations tradition I shall start the explanation of the stemma by describing the Revelations tradition. As may be seen from the preceding chapter, this tradition contains all the Reve- lations MSS (see the list in Chapter 2), with the exception of b and three of the Breviary MSS, viz. Ac Be and Cc. Most of the MSS of the Revelations tradition may be distributed in four groups, (3 X y and a, which will be dealt with in turn (subsections 4.1.1-4.1.4). Then follows a subsection (4.1.5) on the relationship between the four main groups p X y a. The recently discovered MSS Aa Oa Ab j v Fb Jb are dealt with separately in section 4.5. There are also some MSS be- longing to the Revelations tradition which have not been incorporated in the stemma; they are discussed in subsection 4.1.6. In discussing the Revelations tradition, I use mainly the material from the SA text but have derived some benefit also from a comparison with Bergh's edition of Book VII of St. Birgitta's Revelations, since the major part of the SA MSS belonging to the Revelations tradition also contain Book VII and were classified by Bergh. 4.1.1. The r3 group of manuscripts The following MSS belong to the p group: rVYPOeoyaBdmc (for Aa Oa Ab see section 4.5). The p MSS may be divided into two subgroups, f3, (r V Y P) and (32 (0 e o Ya B d m c). In the (32 group there is a configuration termed (0 e o). The relationship between the MSS can be seen from the stemma codicum on p. 22 and has been established in the following way. The relationship between r V Y P (= (31). The four MSS r V Y P form a group and are all derived from a lost MS ((3,; for Aa, see section 4.5). The relation- ship between the four MSS can be seen from the stemma codicum. There are 5 There are the following exceptions. (a) When any MS lacks a major part of the text to which the passage under discussion belongs, this is not mentioned. (b) Occasionally Leby's material (see the Preface) was insufficient and I had to check the MSS myself. Since I did not have access to all of them, I cannot in such cases answer for all of them. This is always marked in parentheses, for example, i: i I aliquid p (=r P 0 B). Whenever I found it necessary to check any of these MSS, this is specifically mentioned, for example, "p1 including Aa" 
 
 
 
 
 26 two discrepancies between my conclusions and Bergh's: he thinks that V was copied from r and takes P to be a a MS, whereas I have entered it in the (31 group. I shall deal first with the relationship between r and V. There are errors in the SA text linking r and V together:6 i: i i ipse Deus r V instead of ipse (ipse om. 0 e); 8: 18 illa r V instead of ipsa sola (sola om. Ad); 15: 2 habebat om. r V; 15: 14 necesse id- V (necesse fuisset obedienciam om. r V. It is evident from the r2) instead of necesse est; 21: 13 in Paradiso datings (r 1399; V 1415) that r is not derived from V and this is confirmed by the omis- sions specific to V: i: 19 virgo ... mater om. V; 14: 6 f. suam nauem nauclerus om. V; 17: 18 non ... sed om. V. These two kinds of errors were found also by Bergh (p. 37) in Book VII and they yield two solutions: either V was copied from r or both r and V were derived from another MS. Bergh has adopted the former alternative, whereas I found it necessary to choose the latter one with regard to the SA material. I start by giving a short account of Bergh's reasoning (pp. 37 f.).7 He has not found any important errors specific to r which are not also in V and this permits the assumption that V was copied from r; yet he describes one minor error specific to r on p. 38 (Rev. VII 5: 26), which, according to him, has been corrected by coincidence or conjecture in V; there is also some difficulty with two of the headings in V (p. 38). But these obstacles to his theory are not serious. He has adduced one fact which, according to him, "in a very high degree supports the idea that V was copied from r". In Rev. VII 31: 6 there is an addition in r and V. The text runs Deinde dixit sponse: "Tu autem dic priori, quod tradat omnia ista verba mea omnium reuela- cionum fratribus et episcopo meo, cui dabo feruorem Spiritus mei ... In r there is an explana- tion of episcopo in the margin: scilicet fratri alfonso olim episcopo genensi, qui dedit nobis locum Janue (locum Janue = the monastery of S. Girolamo di Quarto outside Genoa). In V this notice is incorporated in the text, but the end of it is dropped and it runs scilicet Genensi (with the miswriting alfofonso). Bergh explains this by assuming that the copyist who wrote V used r but abbreviated this notice when he incorporated it into the text be- cause MS V was not to be used at S. Girolamo di Quarto. As far as I can see, however, this is by no means a proof that V was copied from r. It is quite possible that the explanation in question was in an ancestor common to r and V (perhaps in the margin), was handed down in the margin in r in its complete form and was incorporated in its abbreviated form in the current text of V. To sum up, there is nothing in the Book VII material that really argues against the idea that V was copied from r. On the other hand, there is no positive evidence at all for it. Now, the SA material may be taken into account. I have made a special check of all the errors in r to see if they appear also in V and I have found that the following are not in V:8 6 prius r, quamuis prius V instead of quamuis; 3: I I carne virginale r instead of virginale carne; 4: i i Dei claritatis r instead of claritatis Dei; 7: 3 quoque om. r 6 The close relationship between r and V is confirmed also by an observation made by J.-O. Tjäder (NTBB 55, 1968, p. 25), that r was donated to a certain Johannis de Almania, who is prob- ably the same Johannis who wrote Books I—IV in V. 7 According to Westman (p. 264), three copyists worked with MS r. This, however, is not so; as Bergh (p. 36) has pointed out, the first part of the MS (SA) and the last part (Rev. VII—VIII and Ex. 39) were written by the same copyist, the middle part (Rev. V—VI) by another. Thus it would be reasonable to assume that the same stemma applies to SA and Book VII of the Revelations as far as r and V are concerned. 8 I have collected the cases in 3: i i; 4: i i; 9: 17; lo: z; 13: 3; 21: 12; 21: 20; 21: 26 myself and accordingly I cannot guarantee that the same error is not in some other MSS also (see p. 2 4 with note 4). As for the MSS checked for this edition (see pp. 53 f.), none of them has the same error as r in any of these passages. It is, however, of no importance if some other MS should kap- pen to have the same error, since I use them here not as connecting errors but only to exclude the possibility that V is derived from r. 
 
 
 
 
 27 (and 1); 8: 5 denuo siquidem r (siquidem om. 0; for c, see p. 32) instead of siquidem denuo; 9: 17 Deo r instead of Dei (om. F); io: 12 videntur stare r instead of stare videntur; 13: 3 conuallem r instead of vallem; 21: 12 anima r instead of omnia; 21: 20 desiderium angelorum r instead of angelorum desiderium; zi: 26 vobis om. r1, post prestare suppl. r2 (nobis Ad). In my opinion these cases prove that V is not derived from r. In i: 6 the distribution of the readings is hard to understand if it was and this case is best explained by the assumption that there were both quamuis and prius in an ancestor common to r and V. In the other pas- sages it is not possible to assume that V got the correct reading by conjecture, since there was no reason for a copyist to alter the text, nor is it likely that the correct text would have been restored in all these cases by pure coincidence. Thus r and V are descendants from a lost MS, as is shown in the stemma. — There remains the question of the corrections which are in r. They raise various problems, but I shall deal with them only in so far as they are important for determining the relationship between r and V. Some of these cor- rections—but not all of them—are also in V, but in this MS they are in the current text. They may be divided into three groups. (a) First of all, there are the alterations in r which are not also in V. They cannot have been taken from V but are either from another MS or pure conjectures (some of them do not bring about the correct reading but something else which has the appearance of being correct) and they tell us nothing of the relationship between r and V. (b) There are corrections in r, the readings of which are also in the current text of V and restore the correct text in r. Nor do these tell us anything, since they may have been introduced into r from almost any other MS, as weil as from V; to some extent they may also be conjectures. (c) Lastly, there are, however, three more interesting passages. The most significant of them is in 6: z: in V there is an addition of cum after qui (in the current text) and this cum is also in r in the same position but above the line; in r there is also an erasure of creare et and these two words have been altered to creasset. This passage is probably best explained in this way: the cum got into V owing to some mistake; r was collated with V and cum was transferred to it; then creare et in r was changed to creasset, in order to establish the correct syntax. Another case is in 3: 12: a quod which was in the text of the archetype but which is apparently superfluous is missing in V; in r this quod was written first but was later deleted. It is likely that the copyist who wrote V deleted the quod, since lie thought it to be an error; later on, if r was collated with V, it was deleted also in r. But it is also quite possible that two copyists may have deleted it independently. Lastly, there is the following case in 15: 19: instead of animo, there is corde in the current text of V, whereas the writer of r first left the word out and then supplied corde in the margin. In this case also, it is possible that r was collated with V (although there are other possibilities as well): the word animo was missing in the ancestor common to r V, a con- jectural corde was entered in V (or some lost antecedent of it) and transferred to r. To sum up, there is some probability, although no convincing proof, that r was collated with V. There are, however, also alterations in r which were not taken from V and they are either from other MS S or conjectures. It should be added that the corrections in r were probably entered in the MS on more than one occasion.9 None of the corrections in r is of any value in establishing the text. Now, the Y MS also belongs to the f31 group. There are not so many cases in the SA text that link r V Y together, but, since they were taken as a group also by Bergh (pp. 34 f.; cf. also p. 38) on account of the material in Book VII, I have brought them together. The 9 It is desirable that the various hands which have made corrections in r should be identified and classified. It is to be regretted that this cannot be done with sufficient certainty from the micro- film. I have disregarded this problem, since the corrections in r are not important and since my colleague Dr B. Bergh, has checked the r MS in Rome, as regards Book VI of the Revelations, and is to publish the results in a forthcoming book. 
 
 
 
 
 28 following errors are from the SA text: 5: i mundo r V Y (and Ca) instead of eo; 15: 12 parte corporis r V Y instead of corporis parte; 18: 8 ductum r Y (and 0) ductam V instead of perductum. The problem is to find the precise connection between r V and Y. The Y MS cannot be subordinate to the r V group, since r V have errors in common and errors specific to each of them (above) that are not in Y. It would perhaps be possible, however, to take Y in its corrected form to be an antecedent of r V. There is no positive evidence for this in the SA material, but, on the other hand, there is no great obstacle to it—only these three cases in which the correct reading is in r V, whereas Y has minor deviations: 3:Absol. Deus Y (and P) instead of Deum; 4: 9 corruerant Y (and others) instead of corruerunt; 15: i o moriretur Y instead of moreretur. As regards 3:Absol. and 15: i o, the copyist who wrote the lost ancestor of r V may very well have made the conjectures Deum and moreretur on his own initiative, if he copied the text from Y; in 4: 9, however, there is nothing that could have compelled him to change corruerant to corruerunt (though it may have been an unintentional alteration). With regard to these cases and especially to the fact that Bergh has found from the Book VII material that the r V group cannot be subordinate to Y (p. 38), I found it safer not to assume that Y is an ancestor of r V but to adopt the relation- ship shown in the stemma. Now the P MS may be taken into account. Bergh (pp. 59 f.) took it to be a a MS, but I have found it necessary to enter it in the P1 group. First of all, there are some errors common to r V Y P: Prol.: 14 magna r V Y P (and Ea Ca) instead of maxima; 4: 15 maiestas om. r V Y P; 7: 9 ac humanitate r Y P (and C"), hac humanitate V (yet the spelling in V is very curious, also as regards the letter h; cf. Undhagen, p. 5i) instead of et humanitate; 20: 21 ac Dominum r V Y P instead of et Dominum. These errors are not very striking, especially since some of them are also in other MSS, but the tendency is in favour of group- ing P as a 131 MS, together with r V Y. A better proof, however, that this theory is correct is offered by errors common to Y and P. They show a close connection between Y and P and since Y is a Pi MS, it is necessary to take P to be one also. These errors common to Y P have been altered in Y2; in the three following passages it is still possible to see that Y' had the same error as P, whereas the text is correct in Y2: Prol.: 12 Postquam ... con- suatis om. Yl P; 4: 15 tante Y1 P instead of tanteque; iz: i i se ipsam om. Y1 P. In the following cases it cannot be said for certain what there was in Yl, but the MS has an erasure and a correction and it may very well have been the same error which is in P: I: 12 gauderent P (Y1 ?) instead of letarentur; 16: 7 nostris P (Y1?), eorum r V Y2 instead of suis; 12: 17 nos ... recolamus recolant. It may be added in connection with the last passage P (171 ?) instead of hornines that almost all the cases in which it is possible to substitute the first-person plural for the third-person plural have been altered in this way in P and that there are erasures and corrections in Y. Thus Y and P are closely related to each other. It is almost impossible to specify for certain the relationship of P to r V Y, since contamination is involved (be- low) and the solution offered in the stemma is to some extent uncertain. I have found it likely that P is not the ancestor of any other extant MS, since it has errors specific to it, for ex- diuinitas om. P; 8: 11 fideliter om. P; 21: 18 immortales om. P. Nor ample, i: 5 Nam have I taken P to have been derived from Y, although this would, indeed, be possible, since the errors specific to Y are few and unimportant (3:Absol.; 4: 9; 5: i o above), but I think that Y and P are descendants from a lost MS, as shown in the stemma. Yet this reasoning is open to doubt—as I have just said—owing to the contamination. There may be two kinds of contamination. First of all, the errors common to r V Y (above) are not in P; they must have been eliminated in an ancestor of P and this idea is expressed by a dotted line in the stemma. This contamination explains why the errors 
 
 
 
 
 29 common to r V Y P are not striking; they are passages which escaped the attention of the corrector who collated an ancestor of P with some other MS. — There is some probability also that the text common to Y P is contaminated, i.e. that readings were brought into the ancestor common to Y P from some n MS. The following two passages are in favour of this theory. It may be observed in both of them how the readings brought into the ancestor of Y P were eliminated in Y when it was collated with some other MS. In lo: 12 the cor- rect reading (cf. pp. 46 and 66 f.), honorum, was introduced into the ancestor of Y P, in 20: i the incorrect (? cf. p. 71) reading didicimus: lo: 12 honorum Y' P n r honor p (exc. Y' P); zo: i didicimus Y' P n 1" docetur p (exc. Y' P). Interesting in this respect adiutorio Y' P in egressu is also a passage in 15: 24 (cf. pp. 46 and 68): in egressu nostro adiutorio Tt T. cum suo adiutorio p (exc. Y" P) in egressu (in gressu y J') suo eorum In this last case the reading nostro in the ancestor of Y P may have been taken from a lost MS but it may also be an independent alteration made by the copyist who wrote the an- cestor of Y P (cf. the tendency to change from the third-person to the first-person plural, mentioned above). It cannot be said for certain with which of the n MSS the ancestor of Y P was collated, but it might have been a y MSS, since Bergh (pp. 39 f.) found that y readings were introduced into an ancestor of Y, as regarded the Book VII material. On the basis of the above reasoning, I have established the relationship between r V Y P shown in the stemma picture. Since contamination is involved, this solution must not be taken as the only possible one, but I have found it to be the most probable. There are two passages which to some extent support the idea that B was checked against d: in 15: 6 the correct reading is valeret and this is in Ya and B1 (as weil as all the other MSS with the exception of B2 d m); in d there is vellet valeret, whereas m has vellet; in B2 there is an addition of vellet above the line before valeret. The second passage is from Prol.: 4 (cf. pp. 57 ff.): all the ii MSS (and Lc Nc), with the exception of B2 d, have respondebat (the B' reading is difficult to trace but it may have been respondebat), whereas the n MSS and B2 d have respiciebat. In this case it is possible, of course, that the B2 reading was taken from a iv MS, but it may also have been taken from d, if B was collated with d; the reading respondebat was probably introduced into an ancestor of d from the n branch. So much for the collation of B; it is possible that d was collated with B (or perhaps Ya). The following passages argue in favour of such a theory: in 3: 22 there is an addition of corpore seu before vtero in the ya B MSS and this addition is found also in the margin of d. In 19-21:R there is an addition of Maria after virgo in ya B and d2 (also in 1 and c). There are also three cases in which A' B' have the same errors, whereas the correct readings are in Za and CC; this may probably be ex- plained by the assumption that the text in C' has been derived not only from the ancestor common to it and B' and this is expressed by another dotted line in the stemma. There are two passages which may be taken to support this idea, viz. 7: i i (see pp. 4620 and 63) and 8: 23 (see pp. 4620 and 65). In both of them there is one reading (which I have taken to be the correct one) in A' Be C' e TC r and another in Za f3. These passages may be explained by the assump- tion that the r f3 reading is an error which arose in but was corrected in the A' Be C' ancestor—and this means that the A' B' C' text is contaminated. It is, however, also pos- sible that the readings of Za and p are the result of independently committed errors, al- though this seems more unlikely. There is some evidence for this contamination also in the external features of the MSS. The indications of the days are in their normal "Bre- viary" ;position, viz. before the Invitatorium, but all of them are repeated after the Abso- lution before the first lesson of each day in Bc; the same goes for some of the days in A' and C'. This may be explained by the assumption that the SA text in the ancestor of A' B' C' was taken from a Revelations MS, viz. X, and the other parts of the Breviary from a Breviary MS; the indications of the days were retained both in their "Revelations" and "Breviary" positions. Another indication of this kind is that B' (not A' and C') contains the prologue which is generally not in the Breviary MSS. It is possible, then, that the SA text of the A' B' ancestor was taken from a Revelations MS (X) and collated with a Breviary MS; I have indicated this possibility by means of a dotted line. The above argumentation is summed up in the stemma; since contamination is involved, it must not be taken to be the only possible solution, but I regard it as the most probable one. 3 — 714570 Eklund 
 
 
 
 
 34 4.1.3 The y group of manuscripts The following MSS belong to the y group: TEaftXwxzyZXas1hQ (for j v, see section 4.5). The y MSS are related to each other as shown in the stemma on p. 22 and this relationship has been established in the following way. The relationship between T Ea f t. The T Ea f t MSS constitute a subgroup within the y MSS in which Ea f t are derived from T. From the beginning, there were many errors in T, but the MS was corrected and the corrected text is the origin of the Ea f t MSS. This configuration is the same for Book VII (Bergh, pp• 23 ff. and 32). The four MSS are linked together by errors common to them, for instance, an addition of hoc verbum solum after enim in I: 4 and the following omissions: 6: 7 odibiles om. T Ea f t; 13: 4 ab altero vmquam om. T Ea f t; 19: 12 monitrix fide om. T Ea f t (atque roboratrix om. L). MS Ea is derived from T. The following omissions in Ea each correspond to one com- plete line in T (the lines of which are very variable in length): 7: 17 debebas lucide om. Ea; 13: 9 f. consolacionem ... sic om. Ea; zo: 7 ipsi auxilium om. E. Another proof is an omission in 13: 23 f. In T there is the text efficiebantur peccan on one line and the fol- lowing line begins with the rest of the last word, viz. dum; the Ea copyist, however, omitted the first line, except the word efficiebantur, and the E' text runs efficiebantur dum. Lastly, the text of E' in 18: 13 exsiccabant (for the correct exsiccauit) is probably due to a mis- reading by the copyist; in T there is exsiccabat, but the downstroke of -g- in linguam on the line above exsiccabat could easily be mistaken for a linea nasalis above -a- in exsiccabat. There are no significant errors in T which are not also in Ea. The emissions of Ea just quoted (7: 17; 13: 9 f.; 13: 23 f.; 20: 7) and also an omission in i: 21 of et Spiritus ... obedien- cia imply that Ea cannot be the ancestor of any extant MS(S). As regards MS f, the following arguments are in favour of a derivation from T. When T Was corrected, it sometimes happened that the corrector deleted a word in the text and wrote another in the margin; now there are cases in which such words are omitted in f, probably because the copyist did not notice the correction in the margin of T (there are many notes in the margins of T): 7: 12 traxit T1 traxerat (the correct reading; transierat Ad) T2 om. f; 12: 6 venire (the correct reading) T1 demonstrari T2 Ea t om. f; 21: 1 o cominendabant T1 collaudabant (the correct reading) T2 om. f. In 9: 9 there is the name Ade in T; the -d- is written in such a way that it can easily be taken to be an -i- with a linea nasalis; in f this word is written ai e ( = anime). Lastly, there is the same error in 18: 13 that was dealt with above (exsiccabant instead of exsiccauit). The following omissions prove that f is not the original of any other extant MS(S): Prol.: i i Et ... fuit om. f (om. Fl- supra lin. suppl. F2); i: 17 ventorum vmquam om. f; 7: 15 exultabat ...preuidebat om. f; 16: 5 tribulabatur tremore om. f. As regards f, I must explain three passages which are somewhat difficult. In the first two there is an error in T Ea t, whereas f and the other MSS have the correct reading; in the last one there is an error specific to f X w x z y Z X, (and MC Nc), whereas the correct reading is in T Ea t: Prol.: 16 Vt postea T Ea t (and w) instead of Et postea (f and the other MSS); 14: 7 descendunt T Ea t instead of discedunt (f and the other MSS); 17: 16 peperisse f (and XwxzyZXaM' Nc) instead of peperisti (TEat and the other MSS). In Prol.: i 6 the correct reading may very well have been restored in f by conjecture and perhaps the same goes also for 14: 7. In 17: 16 the error may have been committed in- dependently in y and f (its presence in MC N' shows that it could have been committed 
 
 
 
 
 35 independently), but the correct text must have been restored in some way in an antecedent of T or by the T copyist. As regards MS t, there is the following evidence that it is a descendant of T. In t 12: 7 the words et per ... illaberetur, corresponding exactly to one line in T, are omitted. In t the absolution of Chapter i o is placed before instead of after the heading of Chapters i o— i 3; in T this absolution is found in the margin and there is a sign denoting that it should be incorporated in the text before the heading (the absolution is missing in Ea f). Furthermore the word hanc in 13: 18 is missing in t; in T there is a miswriting (perhaps hunc) which has been corrected to hanc in the margin; apparently the copyist did not notice this correction. Lastly, there is the same error in 18: 13 (exsiccabant instead of exsiccauit) which was dealt with above. There are also errors found only in t, proving that t is not the original of any other extant MS(S): i : 6 tangitur seu tangi t instead of tangi; 8: 18 debere procedere om. t; the omission in 12: 7 just mentioned. Thus I have taken t to be derived from T. — There are some passages in which t has the correct reading, whereas there is an error in T E' f but I do not think it is necessary to assume any kind of contamination, since the copyist who wrote t may have made these corrections by conjecture. I give three examples: 9: 14 esset T Ea f instead of est; i i: 8 accendi T E" f instead of ascendere (ascendi y); 21: 5 anima T E' f instead of omnia. Ea f t were copied from the corrected version of T. There are, however, some cases in which the corrections in T were not handed down in E' f t, for example, i: 7 est T' Ea f t (and X w x) instead of eciam (om. 0; eciam is added above the line in T). Cases of this kind may perhaps be most simply explained if we assume that T was corrected on two occasions and E' t f were `ritten before all corrections were incorporated in T; Bergh (p. 25) adduced the possibility that T was corrected on two occasions and based his as- sumption on the fact that not all of the corrections in T are present in f (although, as he also says, the copyist who wrote f may have overlooked some of the corrections in T). The relationship between X w. The X and w MSS are derived from the same MS (viz. x, see below). Also in Book VII there is an ancestor common to X w (see Bergh, p. 32). The two MSS are bound together by the following omissions: 4 litterarum om. X w; 2: 2 nouit Deus fieret om. X w; 6: 6 f. inuidia enim om. X w. In 14: 13, the words quantum ... sinebat are omitted in w and these words are missing also in X but are replaced by non plus; apparently quantum . . . sinebat was missing in the ancestor of X w and the words non plus were introduced to fill up this gap. It is clear from the datings (X 1394 and w 1396) that X cannot be derived from w (there are also errors specific to w, although no striking ones). There are omissions in X that exclude the possibility that X is the origin of w: 1: 8 et ore Filium om. X; 14: 12 nequaquam fuerunt leuati om. X; 17: 14 quem vere sciebat om. X. The relationship between the T Ea f t and the X w groups and the x MS. I shall deal now with the relationship between the group T Ea f t, the group X w and the x MS and refer to the stemma on p. 22 for an illustration of it. It has turned out to be almost impossible to combine the results from the SA and Book VII as regards the position of x. The two groups under discussion and the x MS are bound together by at least one significant error in the SA text: in i: 2 in omnibus ... namque omnibus is missing in all these MSS (in X also the preceding io words et vna ... tres persone, probably owing to homoio- teleuton) and the following two words, eis vna, are transposed as vna eis (this transposition 
 
 
 
 
 36 may be an attempt to produce a more correct text, once the omission was noticed). Beside this case, which seems quite convincing, there are some others, which are, however, not so significant, for example: 1: 7 est T'EaftXwx instead of eciam (om. 0); 3: 9 inseparabiles om. T' X w x (restored in the margin of T2, correct in E' f t; inseparabiliter L). The two groups T Ea f t and X w are apparently in some way descendants from one and the same MS (each of them has errors specific to it, see pp. 34 and 35 above), but the problem is how x should fit into this configuration. It is perhaps most convenient to start by describing the errors in the SA text which are of importance in determining the position of x. First of all, there are errors common to the X w group and x: Prol.: 15 ruminando om. X w x (and Ea b); i: 19 astitisset X w x instead of astitisti; 4: 16 gaudium ex hoc vera- citer honorem et om. X w x (owing to homoioteleuton); 14: 16 nenzinem om. X w x; 17: 4 in eius vtero om. X w x. These passages connect x with X w but it is not possible to take x to be subordinate to the ancestor of X w, since this ancestor had errors specific to it (p. 35 above) which are not in x. This leaves two possibilities: either is x an ancestor of X w or it is derived from a lost antecedent of the antecedent of X and w. I have accepted the former alternative, for two reasons. First of all, there is just one error in x which argues against this idea (see below). Furthermore two of the omissions in X w, viz. 6: 6 f. and 14: 13 (p. 35 above) each correspond exactly to one line in x. So far, the material from the SA is clearly in favour of taking x to be an antecedent of X w, but objections may be raised. First of all, there is one passage in the SA text which does not fit weil into this configuration, viz. 15: 12 non in vna tantum ipsius corporis parte introiens. This correct text is in X w and all the other MS S, with the exception of x, which has non in vna tantum ipsius virginis corporis parte introiens (the addition of virginis was probably due to repetition of a preceding virginis). There is no evident reason why a copyist who used x should have deleted this virginis, but perhaps he found the repetition of the word embarrassing. There are also passages in the Book VII material which argue against my stemma, viz. errors common to x T (Bergh, p. 3o). The passages in Rev. VII 5: 34 and 1o: 18 (Bergh, pp. 3o f.) also raise problems. Nonetheless I have found it justifiable to take x to be an ancestor of X w, espe- cially with regard to the two emissions in X w which correspond to complete lines in x. The embarrassing cases from Book VII must be explained by the assumption that the tradition is not the same for the SA and Rev. VII, as regards the x MS. Lastly I have to describe some errors which compel us to reflect upon the possibility that some lost ancestor of T was corrected, i.e. that the text of T is contaminated. I mean passages in which the correct reading is offered by T (the text of T1, not the corrections) Eaft, whereas X w x z y Z Xa have an error: Prol.: 1 ecclesie sancte sancti Laurencii X w xzyZXa instead of ecclesie sancti Laurencii; 3: 24 quod z y Z Xa, qui X w x instead of quod per; 5: 18 magis creandus X w x z y Z Xa instead of creandus; 15: 6 alia posse X w x y Z Xa aliam posse z instead of talia posse; 15: 8 deusXwxzyZYC instead of dominus; 17: 19 corporibus w x z y Z corporalibus X Xa instead of corporis; 18: 12 virginibus sensibus de- ficientibus X w x z y Z (not Xa) instead of virginis sensibus deficientibus (viribus deficientibus MS). The question here is how the correct readings got into T—if they were taken from some other MS and entered in an ancestor of T, or whether they are only conjectures. In some cases it is reasonable to assume that the correct readings have been restored by conjecture, but it is doubtful whether the other cases are conjectural. I found it safer not to assume any contamination, although this possibility can by no means be ruled out. The relationship between the T Ea f t X w x group and the z MS. Bergh (pp. 28 ff.) did not find any errors that could be used in classifying z, but it can be seen from the SA material that it is derived from a lost MS which is also an antecedent of T Ea f t X w x.

I have taken the source of s 1 h Q to be a lost ancestor of T, as shown in the stemma (which means that T Ea f t X w x z y Z Xa s 1 h Q form the y group; for j v, see section 4.5), but there may also be other solutions. I shall now give my reasons for adopting this classification. Bergh (pp. 65 f. and 69) took s 1 h Q to be a MSS but he also found some kind of con- nection with the y MSS. He pointed out that the group has errors in common with the y MS T (i.e. 'P not T') and gave three possible explanations, viz. (a) that the ancestor common to s 1 h Q was copied from a lost y MS closely related to T (not T itself, since s is older [1424] than T [1437]) and then collated with a a MS, (b) that the ancestor of s 1 h Q was derived from a a MS and collated with a y MS closely related to T, and (c) that readings from the ancestor of s 1 h Q, which was copied from a a MS, were entered in a lost ancestor of T. He found that objections could be raised against all the three alterna- tives but accepted (b) in his stemma, which means that he took s 1 h Q to be primarily a group in the a MSS. In the SA text there are errors common to s 1 h Q and other y MSS. In these parts of the text in s 1 there are errors which are also in T (and other y MSS) and these also support the idea of a connection between the s 1 (h Q) group and the other y MSS (most of them are also obstacles to the (c) theory): Prol.: i Nericie (de) om. s 1 T Ea ftXwxzyZXa; Prol.: 2 preceperat Deus construi slTEaftXwxzyZXa (and b) instead of Christus construi preceperat (Christus preceperat construi o 0 e preceperat Christus construi HC P); Prol.: 5 et altare s 1 T Ea f t (altare N01) instead of a latere; Prol.: 6 dictauit s1TEaftXwxz (and b) instead of dictam; Prol.: 7 ipsa die om. slT1; Prol.: 13 clemen- tissime s 1 T Ea ftXwxzyZ X' (and b') instead of beatissime; Prol.: 16 perhenniter et regnat s 1 T Ea f t instead of et regnat perhenniter; z:Absol. The cases just quoted from the actual SA text, as weil as those from the prologue, the headings and the absolutions, show the connection between the ancestor of s 1 h Q and the other y MSS and they rule out the (c) alternative. It remains to decide from the material adduced whether the (a) or the (b) alternative should be accepted. I have adopted the (a) alternative and have done so for the following reason: there is a pronounced difference between the cases from the actual SA text, which are few and trifling, and the passages from the prologue, headings and absolutions, which are much more remarkable. This is easily explained by the assumption that the ancestor of s 1 h Q was derived from a y MS (an ancestor of T) and later on collated with another MS (maybe a a MS), which lacked the prologue, the headings and the absolutions. In this process, most of the y errors in the actual SA text were deleted, which explains why they are few and trifling in s 1 h Q, whereas they remained in the prologue, headings and absolutions. Since the a MSS have no errors in common (see below), as regards the SA text, it cannot be said for certain whether the ancestor of s 1 h Q was collated with a lost a MS or a lost MS belonging to some other group (maybe a Breviary MS, since these normally do not contain the prologue and the headings). However, the observation made by Bergh, as regards Book VII, that additions have been made in an ancestor of s 1 h Q, which are typical of the 8 grouping, which is a subdivision of a, is in favour of the idea that it was a a MS. 4.1.4. The relationship between the M S q group and the F K 13 Ca MSS ( =a). It is not possible to say from the SA text whether the M S q group and the F K U Ca MSS are to be taken together, and if they are, how they are related; as regards these questions, I have simply copied the conclusions reached by Bergh from the Book VII material (cf. Bergh, p. 69). 4.1.5. The relationship between the 13, X, y and a groups Bergh (pp. 83 if.) found from the Book VII material that y and a are descendants of a lost MS, which he designated it. In the SA text there are no errors at all to show how y and a are related, so I have just accepted Bergh's conclusions. It remains to lind out how f3 X n are related to each other and to the archetype of the Revelations tradition which I have called A. It is necessary, in this discussion, to take into account also the archetypes of the CV (0) and Breviary (1') traditions and for that reason I have postponed the discussion of these matters to the section on the relationship between A e and r. 4.1.6. Other MSS of the Revelations tradition Some Revelations MSS and fragments have not been included in the survey given above for various reasons; they are listed on pp. 12 f. The two others (Copenhagen, Royal Lib., Fragmenter, Pakke 4, and Munich, Bayerische Staatsbibl., Clm. 18361) do not contribute anything to the text; the Copenhagen fragment is apparently closely related to the editio princeps (Ghotan), which in tura means that it is a a MS; as for the Munich fragment, it seems to belong to the P1 group. There are also some complete MSS which were not taken into account. The Celeste Viridarium Tradition The Celeste Viridarium (CV) is a compilation from the Revelations made by Alphonso (p. 2o) and it contains the SA in extenso. However, some passages have been inserted in the SA text which vary in length (a few lines up to several folios) and generally contain dialogue, for example, between St. Birgitta and the angel. The CV is known to me in two MSS L and Ad, and I have collated both of them completely. As can be seen from the stemma on p. 22, they are both derived from a lost MS (0) which is the archetype of the CV tradition. It is not necessary to adduce errors common to L and Ad, since they are already linked together by the fact that the SA in them is part of the CV, with the additions mentioned above within the SA text. Nevertheless I give some examples of the errors they have in common: 7: i i Adam venit L Ad instead of venit; 7: zo produxit L Ad instead of perduxit; 8: 20 quod scilicet Dei L Ad instead of quod Dei; 16: 4 plus dilatatur L Ad instead of se plus dilatat; zo: 19 corporis L Ad instead of cordis. Each of the two MSS has errors specific to it and accordingly neither of them is an ancestor of the other: 8: 7 dando L instead of donando; 9: 4 Deus misericors post hoc L instead of post hec Deus misericors; 13: 5 altitudinem L instead of celsitudinem; 13: 24 aspiciebant L instead of conspiciebant; 17: 7 Quando debebat om. Ad, 18: 4 manibus Ad instead of palmis; zo: 19 facie ad faciem om. Ad; 20: 23 Et ... assumptum om. Ad. 4.3. The Breviary Tradition All the Breviary MSS (see the list on pp. 13 f.), with the exception of Ac Be and Cc, and the Revelations MS b belong to the Breviary tradition. The Breviary tradition is superior to the Revelations tradition in that there are very few errors in the extant MSS (this, of course, does not imply that the text in the archetype of the Breviary tradition was better than that in the archetype of the Revelations tradition). Consequently it turned out to be impossible to produce a detailed stemma (see the stemma on p. 22). The relationship between De Ec. If the f3 X readings were the correct ones in any of the cases in 7: 8, 8: 23 and 14: 2, it would be necessary to alter the judgement passed on some of the passages in i: i i; 8: 6; 8: 15; ED: 12; 14: 15; 15: 24. The best possibility—as far as I can see—is to take the 13 X 0 readings to be correct in all these six cases (not only in I: i i; 8: 6; 14: 15) and to assume the following stemma diagram: f3X r I have rejected this solution for two reasons. First of all, it is necessary to go against the indications from syntax/content in three cases (viz. 8: 1 5; 1 o: 12; 1 5: 24). Secondly, r must be placed on a relatively low level in the stemma and this is not likely to be correct with regard to what we know about the creation of the SA and the Breviary. As I said on pp. 19 f., the translation of the SA from St. Birgitta's native Swedish was probably carried out by Magister Petrus, i.e. the same Petrus who composed the Breviary (p. 20). The fact that one and the same person carried out the transla- tion into Latin of the SA text and composed the Breviary argues against the alternative stemma solution just given, for the following reason. If this was the correct solution, then the MS which Magister Petrus used in composing the Breviary ( = an antecedent of the Breviary archetype r) was at least a copy of a copy of the MS which he produced when he made his translation (viz. A). This is un- likely and it is more probable that Magister Petrus, if he did not keep the original translation (it may, for instance, have remained in the possession of St. Birgitta herself), had at least a copy of it and that there were no more intermediate stages than this. Thus I think that the former solution, i.e. the distribution p X 0 contra 7C 1", is the better one and I have adopted it. This involves the consequence, as I said above, that the 0 7C r readings must be taken to be the correct ones in the cases in 7: 1 i; 8: 23 and 14: 2 and the p Ä readings to be errors linking p and X together.

Recently discovered manuscripts Some MSS have been discovered since the completion of Leby's dissertation (see the Preface), viz. Aa, 0a, Ab, j, v, Fb and Jb.23 I have classified them and in- corporated them in the stemma in the following way. I have checked the errors specific to the MSS of the left- and right-hand stemma branches, in order to see to which branch each of these new MSS belongs. Then I have checked the errors specific to the minor groupings in order to determine—as far as this is possible—the exact position of the MS. The details of the procedure should be clear from the para- graph printed in small type below. It turned out that the Aa, Oa and Ab MSS be- long to the left-hand branch and I start by describing them. The j v Fb and Jb MSS are apparently members of the right-hand branch and are dealt with after Aa, Oa and Ab. The Revelations MS A' seems to belong to the left-hand branch. The Revelations MS Fb seems to belong to the right-hand stemma branch; it has two of the three errors common to the MSS of this branch (i: II; 14: 15 on p. 46); in the third case it has the correct reading (8: 6 propagine) and this is somewhat odd but may be due to pure coincidence. The second possibility is that Fb should be connected at some point above y in the right-hand stemma branch. The only objection to this alternative is that the third possibility seems much more probable and is likely to be the correct one. This third possibility is to include Fb in the a group. The edition has been equipped with an apparatus containing information on 29 The headings in this list, as it appears in the extant MSS, have apparently been handed down from the list which was in Li and have not been brought together independently on two occasions in the two stemma branches from the headings in the SA text (or vice versa), since they differ from them on some points.  55 quotations and reminiscences. Most of them have been taken from other texts and been incorporated in the SA by the translator or by St. Birgitta, but on some occasions the SA text is apparently the source and the appearance of the same words in other texts is secondary. I have not noted this especially in the apparatus, since it is normally evident whether the SA or the other text is primary. Nor do I claim that this apparatus is complete. Then there is also the critical apparatus, which is built up in such a way as to make it possible in each of the parts described above to draw conclusions e silentio, as regards the MSS used in editing this particular part (with the exception of some orthographic details and trifling miswritings; see below). Thus, the information principissima y in the apparatus of Prol.: 1 means that y has principissima and r P 0 B Za x q F principissa (which is in the text). Sometimes, however, the MSS used for each part, as described above, are not enough to establish with certainty the reading of the archetype and other MSS have to be taken into consideration. Passages of this kind may be recognized in the apparatus by the fact that the sign ] (which is used also on other occasions in the apparatus) is then always preceded by notations of MSS which give positive information, i.e. enumerate MSS which have the correct reading. For example, the information in 8: 6 propagine r P 0 B Za Ad q] progenie y x F Fc j° To means that the MSS r P 0 B Za L Ad q have propa- gine, which is the correct reading, the y x F Fc Je Te MSS progenie, which is an error. In cases of this kind no conclusion may be drawn e silentio. Whenever the Greek symbols (pi, 132, etc.) are used in the apparatus, this means that all the MSS belonging to the group, with the exception of the recently dis- covered MSS dealt with in section 4.5, have been checked and have the reading in question (when I found it necessary to check also the recently discovered MSS, this is especially mentioned; cf. p. 255). This rule applies also to the preceding pages of this book (see pp. 24 f.). It should be observed that at least two of the deviations from the correct text in L Ad are deliberate alterations made by Alphonso. When he composed the CV and mixed up parts of the SA with other parts of the Revelations, he changed the text in two of the transitions. The reading Scias quippe quod tercia die of L Ad in 19: 8, instead of Tercia quippe die, was made in order to produce a betteg connection with the preceding text, which is not from the SA. The same is probably true of 19: I I (although L and Ad differ). Corrections in the MSS have been indicated in the following way. When it is clear that the correction was entered by another copyist than the one who wrote the MS, this has been marked by means of index figures, for example, 4: 16 tam om. Zal suppl. Za2. On many occasions, however, it is impossible to say from the micro- films whether a correction was entered by the original writer or not and in that case such information has been omitted, for example, 4: i i ex intuitui in intuitu corr. je. As regards the orthography, I have found it necessary to normalize the spelling in some respects, since the copyists to a very great extent followed their own practice and accordingly the spelling of the archetype cannot be restored for cer- 
 
 
 
 
 56 tain. I have taken the normal ancient classical spelling as my standard and adopted deviations from it3° in the printed text only when a word is often normally spelled in another way in the majority of the MSS, so that there was apparently a more or less established practice. I give a survey here which is intended to serve two pur- poses: it gives a rough idea of what deviations from the normal classical spellings are to be found in the MSS and, since I have generally not described these devia- tions in the apparatus, it shows the kind of orthographic details about which no conclusions can be drawn e silentio from the apparatus. The combination of ch is sometimes used in the MSS instead of h; I have adopted the spelling nichil (for example, 1: 16), which is frequent throughout the entire text, but for other words I have followed the classical mode of spelling. The combination of ci is normally used in the MSS before a vowel instead of ti; I have normalized it to ci (for example, 5: 15 habencia), with the exception, of course, of cases in which the combination is preceded by an s or x. The letter h is sometimes omitted, sometimes inserted; on a few occasions, when the MSS give strong support, I have accepted such deviations (for example, Prol. 16: perhenniter and 8: i i ortulanus) but on others I have printed the classical spelling. The letters d and t are sometimes interchanged at the end of words (for example, 21: 8 sicud F; 6: 3 aput y F) but there is no definite tendency, so I have printed the normal ancient spelling. The letter e is normally used in the MSS instead of the diphthongs ae and oe; this is always the case in the printed edition (for example, 1: I° prescita). Sometimes e is used for i and vice versa; only occasionally has the support of the MSS forced me to accept such a spelling in the printed text (for example, 9: 1 o beniuolum). As regards the letters i and j, I have normalized them to i. Sometimes i is used instead of y and vice versa and this has been accepted on some occasions (for example, 3: 20 liccinus and 6: i Ymmo [this word is spelt with y throughout]). The nasal n is sometimes used for m in the word nanque by the L copyist, but this is not in the printed text or the apparatus. The insertion of p in the combinations of mt and mn has been adopted in passages in which the majority of the MSS support this orthography (for example, 4: 12 V erumptamen). The combination of ph instead off has also sometimes been adopted (for example, 1: 6 nephas). The combination of q and u or the letter q appear sporadically in the MSS for c (for example, 16: I° quotidie L Fe; 14: 12 loquciones F), but this use is never incorporated in the printed text. As regards the letters u and. v, I have followed the practice current in earlier editions of the works of St. Bir- gitta and printed v at the beginning of words and u within the words, irrespective of which of the two letters is really meant (sometimes there is also w in the MSS, for example, i: 1 ewangelio L y, but this spelling is not so well established that it has been adopted anywhere in the printed text). As regards assimilated forms, I have adopted the spellings normally used in the editions of classical texts except when 30 On some occasions the variant is already to be found in the Latin of the ancient periods but is so rare that it is not the normal use, for example, the writing of infrascriptus as one word, which I have accepted in this edition, is to be found in earlier Latin (TLL 7, 1, 1483, 17 ff.) but was ap- parently not the normal way of writing. 
 
 
 
 
 57 there is strong support for some other spelling in the MSS (for example, 5: 15 optinere). The same principle has been applied as regards the use of double/single consonants, as weil as the turning of two words into one (for example, Prol.: 6 infrascriptas; cf. p. 5630). As regards the proper names, I have normalized the name of St. Birgitta (Birgitta) and for other names I have also used one and the same form throughout the entire text, viz. the form which is most frequent in the MSS which the edition is based on. I have not described the various speltings in the apparatus but have given them in parentheses in the Index of persons and places on pp. 143 f. So much for the orthography. There are also some trifling corrections and mis- writings which are not accounted for in the apparatus. Erasures and the blotting out of miswritten letters or words in the current text between two correct words are not mentioned (unless they are important for the stemma construction). In most of these cases the copyist probably made a mistake in writing a word, noticed it immediately himself, deleted it and wrote the correct word. Occasionally the cor- rection may have been made by some other person, for example, in Prol.: 3: the P copyist wrote et ipse twice, but the second et ipse is deleted. I have not mentioned in the apparatus any self-evident corrections of misspellings of single words, for example, 3: 8 ex senerissima in serenissima corr. r. Nor have I bothered to mention simple uncorrected miswritings in one MS which bring about non-existent forms but where it is quite clear what the word intended is, for example 3: 3 pratiarcha 0 instead of patriarcha. 6. Textual criticism In this chapter I have collected all the passages in the text which have to be discussed. I have distributed these passages in three groups on the basis of the degree of uncertainty as to the correct reading. Group i contains cases in which it seems possible to single out one reading as the authentic one by arguments from syntax and/or content, sometimes in com- bination with some degree of probability offered by the distribution among the MSS. Group 2 contains cases in which no arguments can be derived from content or syntax but in which there is some support for one of the readings from the distribu- tion among the MSS, although it cannot be taken for granted that this reading was really in the archetype. Group 3 contains cases in which there are no arguments, either from syntax or content or from the stemma distribution, since there is (with a greater or less degree of probability) one reading at the top of the left-hand stemma branch and another on the right. Group i Prol.: 4 Igitur beata Birgitta habens cameram, cuius fenestra ad altare maius respondebat (respondebat p [exc. B2 d; for Bl B2 see p. 31 Le Nc respiciebat n B2 d), 
 
 
 
 
 58 ende corpus Christi cotidie videre poterat, preparabat se cotidie in eadem camera ad scribendum Since 1 and 0 did not contain the prologue, p. and n represent the tops of the two stemma branches and the choice between the readings which they offer cannot be made from the stemma. This choice must be made entirely by other means. The first question to be answered is whether both the verbs may be used in the sense of "open onto", "face". As regards respicere ad, this sense is listed by Blaise (respicere 1 2). For examples I refer to the indices of Itin.Anton.Plac. and Adamnani De locis sanctis libri tres (both in CSEL 39). There are also several cases in the Vulgate, as can be seen from a concordance, for example, Vulg. Ezech. 43, i Et duxit me ad portarv, quae respiciebat ad viam orientalem. As regards the Middle Ages, it is more difficult to find appropriate passages and I have noted only two in which respicere has the meaning under discussion, although it is constructed with the pure accusative case without ad: Saxo, Gesta Danorum, Praef. 2, 6 provinciae • • • Bootem ... et Arcton respicientes; ibid. 2, I°. As for respondere, I have not found any passage in which it has the meaning required in this case, either in early Latin or in the Latin of the Middle Ages. There is nevertheless some evidence that the word could be used in this way. In the "Deposicio copiosissima" (A&P, pp. 526 f.) there is a revised version of the prologue and there respondebat is used. Apparently the author of this revised text did not take offence at respondebat, since he did not alter it.31 There are also some parallels to be adduced from the Romance languages. 31 It is probable that the version of the prologue found in the Revelations MSS is the original one and that the version of the Deposicio copiosissima has been revised. The prologue was probably written by Alphonso; Collins expresses this idea on several occasions (for example, pp. XVII and xxvi), but I do not really know what this assumption is based on. Westman (p. 24) also assumed that Alphonso was responsible for the prologue of the SA, but the two reasons adduced by him seem very weak. (i) The author of the prologue was not an eyewitness of the revelation of it (and the SA was revealed to St. Birgitta before Alphonso came into the picture). There are, however, also other persons which were not present, for example, Prior Petrus, who was in Sweden. (2) The end of the prologue, with the unctuous admonitions to the nuns of Vadstena, is typical of Al- phonso's style (Westman refers to Steffen [p. 2371], who says that the metaphor in Prol.: 16 is a naive clumsiness and that we do not know who wrote the prologue but that it was probably Alphonso). Westman also compares Alphonso's prologue to the CV without specifying the simi- larities. These two arguments seem feeble, but there is a better one which may have led Collins to the assumption that Alphonso wrote the SA prologue, viz. the heading of the prologue in the Gc MS, which he has edited (Collins, p. 134): The prolog that the deuout fadir alfons some tyme bishop in the kingdome of spayne and aftir hermitt made to the excellence of the blessid virgyn mari endited and geuen to saynt birgitt bi the angel of god. It seems apparent that the meaning is that Alphonso wrote the prologue (this is confirmed by another heading which occurs in the middle of the prologue [Collins, p. 135]: The final conclusion of the prolog and frutful exortacion of the same fadir to the sayd nonnes), but the syntax and exact meaning seem to be in disorder ("a prologue to the excellence" is a curious expression; I suggest that something has dropped out before the ex- perhaps the celestial sermon and legend of (cf. in the middle of the cellence of the blessid virgyn English version of the prologue, Collins, p. 135: And thus and in this vise this celestial sermon and legend of the excellence of the blessed virgin mary was endyted and veriten ...). Thus it is reasonable, I think, to assume that Alphonso wrote the prologue and his text is likely to be the one handed

I have brought together passages listed under dies in the indices of Bergh's and Hollman's editions (and supplied some myself) in which dies is used in the singular and the gender is apparent. The distribution turned out to be as follows: masc.: Rev. VII 6: i; 3i: i i; Ex. 5: i; 17: 7; 88: 2; 104: 5; fem.: Rev. VII 31: 13; Ex. 61: i; 68: I; 96;103: 3;105: 3. This distribu- tion does not tell us very much in itself, but the interesting point here is that in three of the cases (Rev. VII 6: i; 31: i i; Ex. 5: i) with the masculine gender there are alterations to the feminine gender in one or more of the MSS (including the editio princeps, Ghotan), whereas there is no alteration to the masculine gender in the six cases in which the feminine is correct. Although we have in this connection to disregard three of the cases with the feminine gender (Ex. 96; 103: 3; 105: 3) and one with the masculine gender (104: 5), since the text, as regards these, has been handed down only in the editio princeps (Ghotan), there is apparently still a tendency to change from the masculine to feminine gender. This is in favour of Mo in the present case, since it makes it more probable that an Mo was changed to an illa than vice versa. There is also the fact that the expression ipsa die has been used just a little earlier in the text, viz. in Prol.: 7; this is also in favour of Mo, since the alteration may to some extent have been caused by the influence of this preceding feminine die.  6i even more unusual than (ali)quid instead of (ali)quod; he only knows a case in Ulp. dig. and a couple of cases in the writings of Jordanes. It seems to have been rare also in Latin of the Middle Ages for aliquod to be used substantivally. In the MW 1, 460, 54 — 461, 49 there are several examples of substantival use in the neuter nominative and accusative of aliquis; in these passages aliquid is the normal form and there are only four passages with the form aliquod. Yet three of these seem dubious36 and the third, though better, is not beyond doubt: Catal. biblioth. Lehm 116 p. 78, I volumina II garva, unum aliquod ("something to have"), alter nihil. It is, indeed, reasonable to classify aliquod as substantival here, especially on account of the parallelism with nihil, but perhaps one may also say that aliquod is very nearly adjectival ("valuable"). Thus, the substantival use of aliquod was apparently rare also in later Latin and hence it is not very likely that aliquod is the correct reading in the present case. I have also checked those passages in the SA in which the neuter nominative or accusative of aliquis has been used substantivally to see whether aliquod is used on any occasion. There are five passages of this kind (nominative: 3: 2; 8: 23; i o: 6; accusative: 4: 1; 19: 9) and in all of them the form aliquid has been used (4: i ad aliquid creandum mouebatur is especially similar to the passage under discussion). In view of these circumstances, I have taken aliquid to be the correct reading. 2: I Tu quoque, o Maria, ... ab inicio sic eras coram Deo, antequam te creauit (creauit r 0 Za y F Fe Je Te crearet L Ad), sicut archa Noe coram ipso Noe antequam ipsam consummauit (consummauit [aut consumauit] ri-POBZayxqFFer Te consummaret [aut consumaret] r2 L Ad). The question here is whether there should be the perfect indicative or the imper- fect subjunctive in two clauses introduced by antequam. In both the cases there was apparently the perfect indicative at the top of the right-hand stemma branch, whereas p may have had either the perfect indicative or the imperfect subjunctive; this distribution is in favour of the perfect indicative. I have collected other in- stances of antequam in the SA and noted 13 passages with the imperfect subjunctive (I-3:R; 2: 3 bis; 3: i; 3: 2; 3: 12; 3: 20; 4: 19; 8: 16; 8: 23; 9: 12; 17: 20 bis), one only with the perfect indicative (i i: 18) and three with other tenses/moods (3: 21; 3: 24; 12: 6). Thus, the imperfect subjunctive is the normal expression and the perfect indicative comparatively rare (this is the case also in Book VII of the Revelations and in the A&P, see Bergh, p. 53). Accordingly it is more probable that someone should have changed the perfect indicative to the imperfect subjunctive on one occasion (in 0) than that the imperfect subjunctive should have been altered to the perfect indicative on two occasions (in g and Tt [or g).
